Source: Notes of Raft - Laisky
URL: https://blog.laisky.com/p/raft/
Notes of Raft This site requires JavaScript to function. Please enable JavaScript in your browser settings. Skip to content Laisky Posts About AIChat MCP Status RSS × Web Image Sort by Relevance Date Notes of Raft published: 2019-04-23 Raft Changelog: Updated on April 25, 2019: Revised “Cluster Membership Changes” based on doctoral dissertation Updated on April 26, 2019: Revised “Concepts” based on doctoral dissertation Updated on April 28, 2019: Revised “Client Interaction” based on doctoral dissertation Updated on May 5, 2019: Added “Implementation and Performance” based on doctoral dissertation Updated on May 7, 2019: Revised internal cluster communication based on etcd As widely recognized, Paxos is a distributed consensus protocol, but it is also notoriously difficult to understand. During his Ph.D., Diego Ongaro delved into Paxos and proposed the Raft protocol, aiming to provide a more understandable consensus algorithm. The essence of Raft lies in its simplicity, which came at the cost of sacrificing some performance. Fun Fact: Origin of the Raft Name. In short, its name derives from Reliable, And Fault- Tolerant, and is also a metaphor for a life raft that helps you escape from the island of Paxos 😄. Major features of Raft include: a strong leader and unidirectional data flow. Initially, a leader must be elected. This leader is responsible for all transactions, with data flowing solely from the leader to the followers. References: Simplified version (Raft) In Search of an Understandable Consensus Algorithm.pdf Doctoral dissertation (revised, more comprehensive) [Raft] CONSENSUS: BRIDGING THEORY AND PRACTICE.pdf Raft FAQ.txt Raft user study Raft - A Consensus Algorithm for Replicated Logs.pptx https://github.com/etcd-io/etcd/tree/master/raft distributed Serials Time Clocks and the Ordering of Events in a Distributed System Sequential Consistent Notes of "Distributed Systems: For Fun and Profit" Basic Paxos Paper Notes Notes of Raft Consistent Hashing Reading Notes on 'Designing Data-Intensive Applications' The community has widely adopted implementation examples, including: consul etcd tidb Ⅰ、Concepts Consider each node running a state machine and a set of logs. The client submits commands that the state machine executes and, in turn, may change its state. Logs record all commands that could affect the state machine. Any state machine in its initial state can replay these logs in order to restore its final state. Distributed consensus ensures that state machines on each node execute commands in the same sequence and eventually reach the same state. Another way to understand distributed consensus is to ensure the sequence of logs on each node is consistent. However, it is important to note that only the logs “submitted to the state machine” are guaranteed to be in sequence, while some log entries might be tentative and not yet decided to be submitted to the state machine. state machines on a collection of servers compute identical copies of the same state and can continue operating even if some of the servers are down. Replicated State Machines, RSM: A group of nodes in the same state, each with a state machine. Typically implemented as a replicated log. replicated log: A log consisting of commands stored on each machine, to be executed by the state machine in sequence. Each log contains commands in the same sequence, enabling each state machine to execute the same commands and maintain the same state. Consensus algorithm is used to guarantee the consistency of replicated logs across machines. It ensures that the RSM on each node executes the same commands in the same order. Consensus algorithms should satisfy certain conditions: Guarantee consistency under non-Byzantine conditions (without malicious deception); Remain available as long as a majority of nodes are alive; Independent of time, as incorrect clocks and high latency can only cause availability issues, not consistency problems; Results are returned once a majority agrees, unaffected by individual slow nodes. (Note: Majority always refers to a majority of all nodes listed in the configuration, not just the majority of live nodes.) “Non-Byzantine conditions” means that the servers are fail-stop: they either follow the Raft protocol correctly, or they halt. Byzantine failure refers to situations in which some computers execute incorrectly, because of bugs or because someone malicious is controlling the computers. Raft papers point out two major issues with Paxos: Paxos is too hard to understand; The description of Paxos is too vague and far from practical use, resulting in nearly all implementations being modified. The Chubby paper noted that due to the large gap between Paxos description and reality, people tend to implement an unproven Paxos-like protocol. In Raft, all nodes are divided into three roles: leader : There can be at most one leader in the cluster, responsible for initiating heartbeats, responding to clients, and creating log entries. candidate : Transformed from a follower to initiate voting and participate in elections; follower : Only accepts internal cluster communication, participates in voting, accepts heartbeats and data from the leader, and votes for candidates. In the complete papers and etcd implementation, there are a few more intermediate states: learner (non-voting member) : Newly joined nodes without voting rights that need to synchronize data from the leader before transitioning to follower through a confchange. Thus, a learner is not considered a cluster member; pre-candidate : Just initiated an election and is in a temporary state waiting for prevote results. Depending on the prevote results, it may either become a candidate or revert to a follower; Raft requires the election of a unique leader who then assumes full responsibility for managing the replicated log. By having this unique leader, Raft decomposes the consensus problem into three parts: Leader election Log replication: The leader is responsible for receiving requests from clients and disseminating copies throughout the cluster Safety: Ensuring consistency among state machines across nodes Raft divides time into terms, each beginning with an election. That is, each time a RequestVote is initiated, the candidate will increase its term and start voting. Every node maintains a current term , and nodes communicate with each other including this term number . If a node discovers another node with a higher term number, it updates its own term number . If a candidate or leader discovers its term number is not the largest (indicating it has missed some elections), it immediately reverts to the role of a follower. (Lease restrictions will be added later.) If a node receives a request with an outdated term number, such requests are ignored. However, if the node determines that the PreVote mechanism is enabled, it will reject heartbeats and PreVotes, causing the sending leader or candidate to step down to follower. Entry (Log Entry): In Raft, every event is termed an entry, each with an index indicating its position in the log (starting from 1 for ease, allowing prevLogIndex to start from 0). Only the leader can create an entry. An entry's content is <term, index, cmd> , where cmd is an operation that can be applied to the RSM. Once submitted to the RSM, an entry is considered committed. Log: An array composed of entries, with only the leader able to modify the logs of other nodes. Entries are first added to the log (all write operations should be immediately persisted), then consensus requests are initiated, and upon approval, the leader submits them to the RSM. Followers can only obtain leaderCommit from the leader, which is then applied to their own RSM. Raft algorithm guarantees: Election Safety : At most, only one leader exists; Leader Append-Only : The leader's log is append-only; Log Matching : If two logs on different nodes have entries with the same index and term, then those two entries are identical. Leader Completeness : Once an operation is committed, it will be present in the logs in subsequent terms; State Machine Safety : Once a node applies an operation of a certain index to its state machine, all other nodes will apply the same operation of that index consistently. 1、State Persistent state for all servers: currentTerm votedFor : the candidateId that received vote in the current term (null if none); log[] Volatile state on all servers: commitIndex : the highest index known to be committed lastApplied : the highest index applied to the RSM (The reason these do not need to be persistent is that the RSM itself is non-persistent, and the state of the RSM can be reconstructed from log[] ) Volatile state on leaders: (Reinitialized after each election) nextIndex[] : for each follower, the index of the next log entry to send; Initialized to last log index + 1 matchIndex[] : for each follower, the index of the highest log entry known to be replicated Initialized to 0 and increases monotonically 2、Communication Nodes communicate exclusively through three types of RPCs: AppendEntriesRPC : most commonly used for heartbeats or log replication from the leader to followers; RequestVoteRPC : initiated by candidates during the election process; InstallSnapshotRPC : for disseminating snapshots from the leader. In Diego's subsequent doctoral dissertation, additional RPCs were introduced: AddServerRPC : to add a single server; RemoveServerRPC : to remove a server; TimeoutNowRPC : to trigger an immediate election; (In practice, etcd defines dozens of message types, even encapsulating internal events as messages for processing.) AppendEntriesRPC Parameters: term : the current term of the leader; leaderId : the ID of the leader, allowing followers to redirect client connections; prevLogIndex : the index of the log entry immediately preceding the new ones; prevLogTerm : the term of the prevLogIndex entry; entries[] : log entries to be sent to the follower, multiple entries can be sent at once, can be omitted for a heartbeat; leaderCommit : the leader's current committed index, which followers can use to update their RSM. Expected response: term : the term of the responder; success : boolean indicating if the log entries were successfully received. This request notifies followers to commit entries to their RSM through leaderCommit and replicates the leader's log across all followers. Implementation details: Immediately return false if term < currentTerm . Return false if prevLogIndex does not match. If there exists an entry conflicting with the new one (same index but different terms), delete it and all following entries. Append any new entries not already in the log. If leaderCommit > commitIndex , set commitIndex to min(leaderCommit, last index) and commit the corresponding entries. RequestVoteRPC For initiating votes, parameters: term : the candidate's current term; candidateId : the candidate's node ID lastLogIndex : the index of the candidate's last log entry; lastLogTerm : the term of the last log entry. ❓ isLeaderTransfer : indicates that the request is for a leader transfer and must be responded to immediately without waiting for an election timeout. ❓ isPreVote : indicates whether this is a pre-vote or a real vote Expected response: term : the current term of the responder; voteGranted : a boolean indicating if the vote was granted. Implementation details: Return false if term < currentTerm . Grant vote if votedFor is null or candidateId, and if the candidate's log is at least as up-to-date as the voter's log. InstallSnapshotRPC For sending chunks of the leader's snapshot, parameters include: term : the leader's term leaderId : lastIncludedIndex : the index of the last entry in the snapshot; lastIncludedTerm : the term of the last entry in the snapshot; offset : the offset of the chunk; data[] : binary data; done : indicates if this is the final chunk Responses: term : The current term of the follower AddServerRPC Used when changing the cluster configuration file to add a node. Parameters: newServer : Address of the new node Returns: status : bool, indicating whether the addition was successful; leaderHint : Information about the current leader. Implementation details of Raft nodes: If the node is not the leader, return NOT_LEADER ; If not processed within election timeout , return TIMEOUT; Wait for the last configuration change to complete before processing the current change; Add the new configuration item to the log and then initiate a majority consensus. After it passes, commit; Return OK. RemoveServerRPC Used when changing the cluster configuration to remove a node. Parameters: oldServer : Address of the node to be removed Returns: status : bool, indicating whether the removal was successful; leaderHint : Information about the current leader. Implementation details are the same as AddServerRPC . TimeoutNowRPC Initiated by the leader to notify the target node to immediately start an election, ignoring the electionTimeout. Mainly used for leadership transfer. 3、Leader Election Raft uses heartbeats to maintain the identity of the leader. All nodes start as followers. The leader regularly sends heartbeats to all followers to assert its identity. Whenever a follower receives a heartbeat, it refreshes its electionElapsed, restarting the timer. (In the following text, the preset election timeout is referred to as electionTimeout , and the current elapsed election time is referred to as electionElapsed .) Once a follower does not receive any RPC within a specified time ( electionTimeout ), it will initiate an election. After attempting to initiate an election, its status changes to candidate. After increasing its term number, it sends RequestVoteRPC requests to all nodes. The state of the candidate continues until: Winning the election Another node winning the election A round of election ends with no winners The election mechanism is straightforward: whoever obtains the majority of votes ( N/2 + 1 ) becomes the leader. While a candidate node waits for the response to its votes, it may receive a heartbeat from another node claiming to be the leader. In this case, there are two possibilities: The request's term number is the same or greater than its own: This implies the other party has already become the leader, and it immediately reverts to being a follower; The request's term number is less than its own: Reject the request. In etcd, if the candidate receives a RequestVote with a term greater than its own, it also reverts to a follower. (More precisely, it reverts to a follower upon receiving any message with a term larger than its own, except for RequestPreVote and PreVoteResp.) After receiving a RequestVote from a candidate, a follower checks if it has already voted. However, if the request comes from the same candidate, a follower can vote multiple times for the same candidate within the same term. go Copy To prevent too many followers from becoming candidates at the same time, which could prevent a clear majority, Raft employs a mechanism of randomized election timeouts. Each candidate records an election timeout (between 150-300ms) after initiating an election. If the election is not complete after this timeout, the candidate increases its term number and initiates a new round of elections. In such a case, a leader should be determined in a relatively short time. (Because nodes with larger term numbers have a greater chance of overcoming others) In etcd, the randomized election timeout is set to [electiontimeout, 2 * electiontimeout - 1] . If a leader cannot complete a heartbeat with a majority of nodes within the electionTimeout, it is likely that the leader has lost contact with the cluster. Therefore, the leader should return a failure to all client requests and revert to a follower. (Section 6.2) Safety Restrictions Due to the dominant position of the leader, Raft ensures during the voting phase that the elected leader contains all the logs that have been committed across the entire cluster up to that point. When a candidate sends a RequestVoteRPC, it includes information about the last entry. Upon receiving this request, all nodes compare it with their own logs. If they find their log to be more up-to-date, they will refuse to vote for that candidate. (The same applies to prevotes; if a follower considers a precandidate unqualified, it will refuse to prevote.) The method for determining which log is more up-to-date involves comparing the last entry in the requesting node's log to the last entry in their own log. First, they compare the term; if their own term is greater, they refuse the request. If the terms are equal, they then compare the index; if their own index is greater (indicating a longer log), they refuse the request. go Copy As illustrated above, to avoid consistency issues, Raft mandates that the leader never commits entries from past terms (even if those entries have been replicated to a majority of nodes). The leader only commits entries from the current term, and past entries are only committed alongside current entries (as in the case with c, where entries from term2 are only committed along with those from term4). If a candidate obtains a majority vote, it indicates that its logs are the most complete among the majority of nodes. Therefore, it can be deduced that the candidate contains all the committed entries of the entire cluster. (After being elected, the leader should immediately issue an AppendEntriesRPC with a no-op entry, as discussed later.) 4、Log Replication Once elected, the leader handles all client requests. Each client request contains a command to be executed by the RSM. Upon receiving a client request, the leader generates an entry containing <index, term number, cmd> . After appending this entry to its own log, the leader broadcasts this entry to all nodes. If a follower agrees to accept this entry, it adds the entry to its own log and replies with its agreement. If the leader receives successful responses from the majority of nodes, it applies the entry to its RSM and thereafter declares the entry as committed. This committed status is conveyed to the other nodes via AppendEntriesRPC. Raft guarantees two properties: If two entries in different logs have the same index and term, they must have the same command. If two entries in different logs have the same index and term, all preceding entries in both logs must also be identical. The first property is ensured by allowing only the leader to generate entries, and the second is secured through a “consistency check” which includes the following steps: When communicating with a follower via AppendEntriesRPC, the leader includes the information of the preceding entry. The follower compares this with its own log, and if there's a discrepancy in the entry's information (index or term), it will reject the request. If a leader discovers a follower has rejected a request, it conducts another round of consistency checks with that follower, identifying the largest point of agreement and then overwriting the follower's subsequent data with the leader's entries. To find the point of agreement, the leader continues to send previous entries via AppendEntriesRPC. If the follower continues to reject them, the leader sends even earlier entries until an agreement is reached. Given that disparities are rare and usually quickly resolved, this block-by-block confirmation generally doesn't result in performance issues. Each leader keeps a nextIndex variable for each follower, indicating the index of the next entry to be sent to that follower. Initially set to the leader's log index + 1 when a leader is elected, nextIndex is decremented ( nextIndex-- ) and the entry is resent whenever a follower rejects an entry. 5、Follower and Candidate Crashes The discussion above mainly covers handling leader crashes; handling crashes of followers and candidates is simpler. Since all Raft RPCs are idempotent, all requests in Raft are retried indefinitely in case of timeout. Followers and candidates that recover from crashes can receive new requests and respond to them by appending the entry or rejecting it, as discussed above. 6、Persistence Nodes in a Raft cluster need to persist certain data. Data that must be persisted includes: current term vote: records of current round voting to prevent double voting new log entries that have not yet been committed (TODO: Generally, this involves persisting terms, votes, snapshots, and all logs.) The state machine (RSM) may choose to persist or not. If not persisting the RSM, then snapshots and all logs following the snapshot must be persisted. If a node loses too much critical data, it cannot continue operating with its previous ID. Instead, it should be added back to the cluster as a new node using cluster update algorithms. 7、Timing and Availability Raft can ensure consistency in most delay scenarios. However, for optimal selection and leader operation, it is best to meet the following time conditions: text Copy broadcastTime : average response time for broadcasting a message to other nodes; electionTimeout : the duration a follower waits before assuming a leader failure (the longest tolerable heartbeat interval); MTBF(mean time between failures) : average healthy operating time of a single machine; Generally, broadcastTime is around 0.5 to 20ms (including the time for disk persistence), and MTBF is usually a few months. electionTimeout can be set between 10 to 500ms. According to the literature, even when one-way delays reach 30 to 40ms, 99.9% of elections can still be completed within 3 seconds. 8、Leadership Transfer Sometimes, it may be necessary to relinquish the current leader's control, such as: The leader node requires a restart for maintenance reasons; Another node is better suited to serve as leader. Shutting down the leader node directly would cause other nodes to wait until electionTimeout is reached before entering election mode, rendering the cluster unresponsive during this period. To avoid this downtime, a leadership transfer mechanism can be employed. The steps for a leadership transfer are: The leader stops responding to client requests; The leader initiates log synchronization with the target node; The leader sends a TimeoutNowRPC to the target node, which, upon receipt, immediately starts a new election. In etcd, more detailed steps (with some modifications) are implemented: The leader first verifies the identity of the target node ( leadTransferee ). If it is a follower, the request is ignored; The leader checks if a transfer is already in progress. If so, it aborts the previous state and processes the latest request; It checks whether the target node is itself. If so, the request is ignored; The leader records the transfer status information in its state and resets electionElapsed (as the transfer should be completed within electionTimeout ); It checks whether the target's log is up-to-date: If the target's log is already up-to-date, the leader sends a TimeoutNowRPC directly; Otherwise, the leader sends an AppendEntriesRPC. Once the target node successfully responds, the leader sends a TimeoutNowRPC. Etcd implementation highlights that aside from resetting electionElapsed , the leader does not alter its status. It continues to respond to client requests and send heartbeats while the transfer is in progress. Since the target machine updates its term and initiates the election first, it has a high probability of winning the election. Note that the RequestVoteRPC sent by the target includes isLeaderTransfer=true to prevent other nodes from ignoring it. If the target machine fails to complete the election within one electionTimeout , the leader assumes the transfer has failed and immediately resumes responding to client requests. (A new transfer request can be initiated at this point.) In the etcd/raft implementation, the RequestVoteRPC.context is set to campaignTransfer , indicating the vote request stems from a leadership transfer, allowing it to forcibly break followers' leases to start an election. 9、Pre-Vote A node temporarily disconnected from the cluster network can disturb the cluster's operation upon rejoining. After losing contact with the cluster and waiting for electionTimeout , such a node would increase its term and initiate an election. Being unable to reach other nodes, it would continue to increase its term after each electionTimeout and attempt further elections. Eventually, its term could significantly exceed that of the original cluster. If this node were to reestablish contact with the cluster afterward, its high term would cause the current leader to step down, triggering a new election. To avoid this scenario, a Pre-Vote mechanism is introduced. Under this mechanism, a candidate must obtain a majority agreement before it can increase its term. A node will only agree to a candidate under the following conditions: The candidate's log is sufficiently up-to-date; The current node has lost contact with the leader ( electionTimeout ). In other words, a candidate will first initiate a round of pre-vote, seeking majority approval before updating its term and then initiating a RequestVoteRPC. In this situation, a node disconnected from the cluster will only initiate pre-vote without updating its term. In etcd's implementation, a node agreeing to a candidate does not need to update its state and can still agree to other candidates. Even if the PreVote's term is higher than its own, the node will not update its term. Thus, PreVote does not alter the state of other nodes. Etcd also designs PreVote such that it targets the next term. Therefore, it sends a PreVoteReq with term+1 to all nodes. go Copy Ⅱ、Cluster Node Changes There are two methods of making changes: Changing multiple servers at once through joint consensus . [Recommended] Changing one server at a time with the single-server configuration change algorithm . 1、Single-server configuration change algorithm In the Raft algorithm, as every node in the cluster holds information about the entire cluster, changes in cluster membership (addition or deletion of nodes, switching nodes, etc.) can occur. Raft limits changes to adding or deleting one node at a time, and only after one change is completed can another commence. Changing one node at a time simplifies the process to just requiring a majority ( N/2+1 ) in both the old and new cluster configurations. As a result, there will always be an overlap between these two majorities, ensuring the system is protected against splits caused by inconsistent configurations. When a leader receives a request for a cluster change, it generates a special entry to store the configuration. As soon as a configuration entry is added to the log, it becomes active (meaning any node that receives the new configuration immediately adopts it). The leader then disseminates this entry to a majority of nodes, and upon success, commits the entry. Once a new configuration item is committed, that change is considered finished, and processing of the next change can begin. For availability purposes, a rule is added: nodes should handle RPCs without considering whether the source node is in their configuration file. In other words, even if an RPC comes from a node not in their configuration file, nodes should process and respond to it normally. This includes AppendEntriesRPC and RequestVoteRPC . 2、Joint Consensus This method allows changing multiple nodes at once (arbitrary configuration). To ensure service availability (without interruptions) and avoid consistency issues caused by node changes, Raft introduces a two-phase change. Upon receiving a new configuration file, the cluster first enters a joint consensus state, and once the new configuration file is successfully committed, transitions back to regular operation. More specifically, joint consensus refers to an intermediate state including all nodes from both the new and old configuration files: Entries are replicated to all nodes in the new and old configuration files. Any node from the new or old configurations can potentially be elected as leader. Consensus (for elections or commits) requires obtaining separate majorities in both the new and old configurations. Separate majorities mean that a majority is needed from both the old and new clusters. For example, if the switch is from 3 to 9 new nodes, it requires agreement from 2 old nodes and 4 new nodes to be considered a consensus. Thus, a configuration change goes through three states: C_old : Using the old configuration file. C_old,new : Using both the old and new configuration files, essentially the union of old and new nodes. C_new : Using the new configuration file. Configuration files are stored using special entries. As soon as a node receives a new configuration file, it immediately adopts that configuration, even if the corresponding configuration entry has not been committed. So, a complete configuration change can be visualized as follows: C_old,new is created, and the cluster enters joint consensus , with the leader beginning to propagate the entry. C_old,new is committed, meaning a majority of nodes now have C_old,new , and C_old can no longer elect a leader. The leader creates and propagates C_new . Once C_new is committed, nodes not in C_new are not allowed to be elected as leader, and any such leader must step down. 3、Considerations References: https://etcd.readthedocs.io/en/latest/server-learner.html The following considerations apply to both algorithms described above. Catching up new servers New nodes without historical logs may take significant time to catch up. Introducing empty nodes without logs can create availability gaps due to synchronization delays. One solution is to initially classify new nodes as non-voting members , unable to vote or contribute to the majority needed for elections, until synchronization with the leader is complete. The leader must decide when a new node is ready. If the leader believes a new node is too slow or may never catch up with the log progress, it should abandon the new node and consider the change attempt failed. Upon receiving a new node configuration, the leader focuses on log synchronization. Only after successful synchronization does the leader create a configuration entry and execute the change. If synchronization fails, no such entry is generated, meaning a non-voting member is technically not part of the cluster until after successful synchronization. (etcd currently doesn't implement automatic node addition; manual addition of a learner is needed until synchronization is complete, with manual promotion expected in v3.4 to introduce a default learner mechanism.) The paper suggests a simple method for determining if a new node is ready: when the leader begins synchronizing logs for the new node, a maximum number of synchronization rounds is preset. Each round consists of all the new logs currently held by the leader. During this process, the leader continues to receive new log entries intended for the next round of synchronization with the new node. If the synchronization time falls within the electionTimeout during the preset final round, the node is deemed ready. If the final round times out, the leader rejects the change. The initiator may retry, and given that the node already possesses some logs, synchronization should be significantly faster and more likely to be accepted by the leader. (TODO: A question remains: given the first round of synchronization is likely to take the longest, should there be a timeout check or limit placed on the length of the first round? Moreover, should synchronization begin with a snapshot before proceeding with the logs? Etcd's approach does not count rounds for synchronization and uses the standard AppendEntriesRPC without keeping track of rounds.) How does a leader determine the number of logs a new node has? The simplest method is back-and-forth during consistency checks, but this can be time-consuming with lengthy logs. An optimization is for a follower to report its log length when responding to AppendEntriesRPC , allowing the leader to more quickly ascertain the nextIndex . Removing the current leader The leader might not be in the new configuration. In the original paper, the leader continues to function until C_new is committed. Once C_new is committed, any leader not in C_new immediately steps down to a follower position. During attempts to commit C_new, a leader not in C_new does not participate in voting. In his doctoral dissertation, upon discovering it's not in the new configuration and after C_new is committed, a leader can employ the leadership transfer mechanism discussed in section 3.10 (p46) to relinquish control. Both approaches lead to two peculiar scenarios: A leader might manage a cluster it's not a member of. A server might participate in an election or even become a leader without being in its own configuration file. Disruptive Servers Nodes not in C_new could disrupt the cluster. Once C_new takes effect, removed nodes will stop receiving heartbeats, causing them to update their term and attempt to start an election after electionTimeout. This forces the current leader into follower mode and triggers an election, a cycle that can significantly reduce efficiency. A solution is for each follower to incorporate a mechanism that, while within the minimum election timeout and believing its leader is still active, refuses RequestVoteRPC. It won't agree to vote or update its term based on RequestVoteRPC. However, if the cluster is in the midst of an election, nodes from C_old could still cause interference. Thus, combining this with PreVote is more reliable. This mechanism could interfere with the leader transfer process, where RequestVoteRPC should interrupt all nodes to initiate an immediate election, regardless of electionTimeout. Therefore, RequestVoteRPC should include a flag to indicate if it stems from a leader transfer. Etcd also adds a campaignTransfer flag to signal this special case. 4、System Integration Generally, it's not recommended to automatically take down faulty nodes through monitoring. A better practice is to launch a new node to replace the faulty one if deemed necessary. When employing a single-node change strategy, it's recommended to add the new node first and then take down the old node. Ⅲ、Log Compaction When there are too many log entries, synchronization between nodes can become overly time-consuming. The simplest optimization is to regularly create snapshots. Snapshots include: The current state of the RSM (replicated state machine). The index and term of the last entry (to comply with other RPC request parameters). Current cluster configuration information. Each node decides when to create its own snapshot. If the leader finds the nextIndex for a follower has been included in a snapshot, it needs to send the snapshot to that follower. Upon receiving a snapshot, the follower must determine: If the snapshot is ahead of its log, use the snapshot to completely replace all existing logs. If the snapshot is behind its own log, use the snapshot to replace the relevant part of the log while retaining subsequent logs. Snapshots can raise two issues: When to take snapshots? A straightforward strategy is to set a fixed maximum disk capacity threshold. Trigger a snapshot once the log exceeds this capacity. Snapshotting the RSM will impact new update operations. It's advisable to employ copy-on-write operations to minimize the impact on new updates. Ⅳ、Client Interaction The client refers to users outside the Raft cluster who initiate requests to the Raft cluster. 1、Requests Interactions between the client and the Raft cluster are done through RPCs. Clients should: Locate the cluster; Find the leader; Register a session; Initiate requests; Retry requests. This includes three types of requests: ClientRequestRPC RegisterClientRPC ClientQueryRPC ClientRequestRPC Requests initiated by the client to the Replicated State Machine (RSM). Parameters: clientId : The session ID obtained through RegisterClientRPC ; sequenceNum : A unique ID for each request, ensuring idempotency; command : The command to the RSM. Returns: status : Whether the operation was successful; response : The output of the RSM; leaderHint : Address information to redirect to the leader. Implementation details: If the node is not the leader, return NOT_LEADER . Provide leader information if possible; Generate an entry for the request, add it to the log, and replicate it across the nodes; If the sequenceNum has been discarded (❓TODO: I interpret this as being less than the minimum sequence number stored for the session), or the clientId is not on record, return SESSION_EXPIRED ; If the sequenceNum has already been processed, return OK directly; Execute the command to the RSM in the order of the log; Save the output of the RSM along with clientId and sequenceNum , discarding the previous output; Return OK. RegisterClientRPC For new clients, register a session once. Returns: status : Whether it's ok; clientId : Newly allocated clientId; leaderHint : Information to redirect to the leader. Implementation details: If not the leader, return NOT_LEADER . Provide leader information if possible; Generate an entry for this registration, add it to the log, and replicate it across the nodes; Execute the command in the order of the log to create clientId ; Return OK and the clientId . ClientQueryRPC Read-only requests initiated by the client. Parameters: query : A read-only request to the RSM (does not change the state of the RSM); Returns: status : Whether it's ok; response : The output of the RSM; leaderHint : Information to redirect to the leader. Implementation details: If not the leader, return NOT_LEADER . If possible, provide leader information; Wait for the last committed entry to achieve consensus; Save commitIndex as readIndex ; Initiate a round of heartbeat, waiting for the majority to return; Wait for the RSM to execute up to readIndex ; Execute the request (read-only request to the RSM); Return OK , as well as the output of the RSM. 2、Cluster Discovery To ensure clients can contact a valid node of the Raft, all node information can be stored in DNS. DNS does not need to maintain strong consistency with the nodes, as long as it contains a sufficient number of valid Raft nodes. Clients can continue trying until they contact a live node. Clients only send requests to the leader. However, upon startup, clients need to undergo the following steps to find the leader: When the client starts up, it will randomly connect to a Raft node. If this node is not the leader, it should return information about the leader to the client. After obtaining the leader node's information, the client tries to contact the leader again. If the leader has crashed, causing a connection timeout, the client should try contacting a random node again and repeat the first step. To prevent operation repetition, clients should generate a globally unique sequence number for each write request. If a request times out, the client can immediately retry. Raft nodes should save the most recent effective sequence number for each client. If a repeated submission is detected, it will be ignored. However, each node may store outdated information: Leaders: A leader that has lost communication with the cluster might mistakenly believe it is still the leader. Therefore, all client RPCs mentioned above ensure consensus is reached before responding, to confirm the node is still the leader. If a leader cannot reach consensus within an electionTimeout , it automatically demotes itself to a follower. Followers: Followers might save incorrect leader information. Whenever a new term is updated, followers must discard previously stored leader information. If the client cannot contact the leader after obtaining the leaderHint , it should randomly contact another node. Clients: In case of a disconnection with the raft leader, do not attempt to reconnect but choose another random node. 3、Linearizability Semantics Linearizable consistency, also known as strong consistency or atomic consistency, needs to satisfy: Any read operation will reflect the result of the most recent write operation; The sequence of operations observed by all processes is consistent under a global clock. ❓TODO: Personally, I feel that linearizable consistency is sequential consistency + sync? Taking Raft as an example, the nodes are sequentially consistent, and the leader and heartbeat mechanism are used to achieve linearizable consistency. If a leader has reached consensus on a client request but crashes before responding, the client may attempt to retry the request. This could lead to the request being executed twice (at least once). To avoid the above problem and achieve linearizability, Raft needs to ensure that each node can recognize and filter duplicate requests. We have already set a unique sequence number for each request, so the state machine of each node saves a session for each client. The session records the sequence number of the last command processed for that client. The above method can also apply to clients concurrently issuing requests. The server saves all sequence numbers and results of the session, and the client includes the earliest unreturned request sequence number with each request. The server returns requests after this sequence number, discarding all requests before it from its cache. However, space is limited, so sessions must invariably be terminated, and all nodes must reach consensus on when to expire each session. Session expiry must be deterministic There are two viable approaches: Set a maximum limit for sessions. When the number of sessions exceeds this limit, use an LRU algorithm to expire old sessions; Set a timeout for sessions. Clients regularly update sessions through heartbeats. The leader adds the local time into the entry for propagation, and nodes use this time to refresh client sessions. ❓TODO: Does this mean that every entry must contain the session id, command id, and current time? And it also needs to be spread throughout the entire cluster, with each node maintaining a session table? Then, what happens to the session table when the leader changes? Another issue is, if a session has been expired but the client continues to send requests, what happens? The simplest approach is to return an error asking the client to re-register the session. ❓TODO: There's another question: if a client makes a request, and then the leader crashes, can the client apply for a new session and retry the command of the previous session? In other words, the client design should be based on sessions. (Could this be implemented like ZooKeeper's ephemeral node?) 4、Rapid Response to Read-Only Requests For write operation requests, consistency is guaranteed by requiring a majority consensus. However, read operations present a possibility where the leader might return outdated data (if there is a new leader that the current leader is unaware of). To ensure consistency in handling read-only requests, two additional steps are necessary: The leader must possess all committed entries Upon being elected, a leader initiates a consensus request for a no-op entry that contains no actual request; this is to ensure that the leader holds all committed entries and maintains consistency among followers and itself. The leader must verify its ongoing leadership Before responding to read-only requests, a leader needs to initiate a round of heartbeat checks and receive responses from a majority of members to confirm its current leadership status. A valid leader, by definition, contains all committed entries. Hence, the comprehensive steps for responding to read-only requests are: The leader ensures that it has completed at least one submission in the current term; if not, it initiates a consensus for a no-op entry. Record the index of the last committed entry into the local variable readIndex . Initiate a round of heartbeat checks and receive the majority's responses. Wait until the Read-State Machine (RSM) executes up to readIndex . Respond with the results from the RSM. Optional optimizations include: Followers responding to read-only requests Upon receiving a read-only request, a follower requests the readIndex from the leader, who then executes steps 1 to 3. Following this, the follower executes steps 4 to 5. (However, this method increases complexity and requires careful implementation.) Batch processing of read-only requests When responding at step 5, all read-only requests received between steps 1 to 5 are responded to at once. This scheme is also applicable to followers. Lease-Based Optimization In the schemes mentioned above, a read-only request could trigger a heartbeat across the cluster. To enhance efficiency and avoid extra requests, optimizations based on leases can be used. After a leader confirms a round of heartbeat, it can assume there will be no other elections within the electionTimeout period, allowing direct responses to read-only requests without additional heartbeats. However, it's essential to consider that a leader might initiate an election within a period less than electionTimeout during leadership step-down, hence the need to revoke the lease (setting electionEclaps to 0) before stepping down. This requires that the clock drift bound between services isn't too significant and they are unaffected by NTP adjustments. After each heartbeat, the lease can be extended to start + electionTimeout/clockDriftBound . A straightforward optimization is for servers to include the most recent log index when responding to clients, who, in turn, include the last known log index in their requests. Generally, the client's index should be equal to or less than the server's index. If a server finds its index lagging behind the client's, indicating a loss of cluster connectivity, it should refuse the request. Ⅴ、Implementation and Performance 1、Multithreading A lock-based monitor is used to maintain a shared state, with the state machine and logs being operated in a lock-free single thread, and other functionalities delegated to a thread pool. Modification of the state requires acquiring the lock on the monitor. The primary components include: Peer threads : A thread is created for each node in the cluster to facilitate internal communications; Service threads : A thread pool for responding to client calls and internal cluster communications; State machine thread : A single thread to maintain the state machine; Timer threads : Timers for non-leaders ( electionTimer ) and leaders ( timeoutTimer , transitioning to follower upon timeout); Log Sync thread : A single thread dedicated to persisting logs. 2、Performance Optimization Generally, the most significant time costs involve persisting logs and replicating them within the cluster. Parallel Writing In its simplest form, a leader persists an entry before initiating consensus within the cluster. However, each follower also needs to complete persistence before responding, resulting in twice the persistence time for each consensus. A simple optimization is for the leader to simultaneously perform disk sync and initiate consensus. Commit is executed once a majority of followers have responded, potentially even before the leader's sync is completed. Batching and Pipelining Log persistence can be optimized through batch processing, where multiple entries are persisted at once. Log replication can also use batch processing, where each AppendEntriesRPC carries as many entries as possible (under 1MB) to reduce the number of RTTs. The 1MB limit is based on experience. AppendEntriesRPC can further be optimized with pipelining. For pipelining to be effective, several improvements are necessary. After sending an entry, the leader immediately updates the nextIndex and starts processing the next batch of entries before receiving any response. This means that the leader does not strictly send entries in order but relies on the follower's consistency checks to ensure correctly ordered log entry addition. Under TCP, the majority of log entries will arrive in order, with any inaccurately arrived entries triggering a nextIndex rollback due to failed consistency checks. From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia Theorem in theoretical computer science The tradeoff between availability, consistency and latency, as described by the PACELC design principle. In database theory, the PACELC design principle is an extension to the CAP theorem. It states that in case of network partitioning (P) in a distributed computer system, one has to choose between availability (A) and consistency (C) (as per the CAP theorem), but else (E), even when the system is running normally in the absence of partitions, one has to choose between latency (L) and loss of consistency (C). Overview [ edit] The CAP theorem can be phrased as "PAC", the impossibility theorem that no distributed data store can be both consistent and available in executions that contains partitions. This can be proved by examining latency: if a system ensures consistency, then operation latencies grow with message delays, and hence operations cannot terminate eventually if the network is partitioned, i.e. the system cannot ensure availability. [1] In the absence of partitions, both consistency and availability can be satisfied. [2] PACELC therefore goes further and examines how the system replicates data. Specifically, in the absence of partitions, an additional trade-off (ELC) exists between latency and consistency. [3] If the store is atomically consistent, then the sum of the read and write delay is at least the message delay. In practice, most systems rely on explicit acknowledgments rather than timed delays to ensure delivery, requiring a full network round trip and therefore message delay on both reads and writes to ensure consistency. [1] In low latency systems, in contrast, consistency is relaxed in order to reduce latency. [2] There are four configurations or trade-offs in the PACELC space: PA/EL - prioritize availability and low latency over consistency PA/EC - when there is a partition, choose availability; else, choose consistency PC/EL - when there is a partition, choose consistency; else, choose lower latency PC/EC - choose consistency at all times PC/EC and PA/EL provide natural cognitive models for an application developer. A PC/EC system provides a firm guarantee of atomic consistency, as in ACID, while PA/EL provides high availability and low latency with a more complex consistency model. In contrast, PA/EC and PC/EL systems only make conditional guarantees of consistency. The developer still has to write code to handle the cases where the guarantee is not upheld. PA/EC systems are rare outside of the in-memory data grid industry, where systems are localized to geographic regions and the latency vs. consistency tradeoff is not significant. [4] PC/EL is even more tricky to understand. PC does not indicate that the system is fully consistent; rather it indicates that the system does not reduce consistency beyond the baseline consistency level when a network partition occurs—instead, it reduces availability. [3] Some experts like Marc Brooker argue that the CAP theorem is particularly relevant in intermittently connected environments, such as those related to the Internet of Things (IoT) and mobile applications. In these contexts, devices may become partitioned due to challenging physical conditions, such as power outages or when entering confined spaces like elevators. For distributed systems, such as cloud applications, it is more appropriate to use PACELC, which is more comprehensive and considers trade-offs such as latency and consistency even in the absence of network partitions. [5] History [ edit] The PACELC design principle was first described by Daniel Abadi from Yale University in 2010 in a blog post, [2] which he later clarified in a paper in 2012. [3] The purpose of PACELC is to address his thesis that "Ignoring the consistency/latency trade-off of replicated systems is a major oversight [in CAP], as it is present at all times during system operation, whereas CAP is only relevant in the arguably rare case of a network partition." PACELC was proved formally in 2018 in a SIGACT News article. [1] Database PACELC ratings [ edit] Original database PACELC ratings are from. [6] Subsequent updates contributed by wikipedia community. The default versions of Amazon's early (internal) Dynamo, Cassandra, Riak, and Cosmos DB are PA/EL systems: if a partition occurs, they give up consistency for availability, and under normal operation they give up consistency for lower latency. Fully ACID systems such as VoltDB/H-Store, Megastore, MySQL Cluster, and PostgreSQL are PC/EC: they refuse to give up consistency, and will pay the availability and latency costs to achieve it. Bigtable and related systems such as HBase are also PC/EC. Amazon DynamoDB (launched January 2012) is quite different from the early (Amazon internal) Dynamo which was considered for the PACELC paper. [6] DynamoDB follows a strong leader model, where every write is strictly serialized (and conditional writes carry no penalty) and supports read-after-write consistency. This guarantee does not apply to "Global Tables [7]" across regions. The DynamoDB SDKs use eventually consistent reads by default (improved availability and throughput), but when a consistent read is requested the service will return either a current view to the item or an error. Couchbase provides a range of consistency and availability options during a partition, and equally a range of latency and consistency options with no partition. Unlike most other databases, Couchbase doesn't have a single API set nor does it scale/replicate all data services homogeneously. For writes, Couchbase favors Consistency over Availability making it formally CP, but on read there is more user-controlled variability depending on index replication, desired consistency level and type of access (single document lookup vs range scan vs full-text search, etc.). On top of that, there is then further variability depending on cross-datacenter-replication (XDCR) which takes multiple CP clusters and connects them with asynchronous replication and Couchbase Lite which is an embedded database and creates a fully multi-master (with revision tracking) distributed topology. Cosmos DB supports five tunable consistency levels that allow for tradeoffs between C/A during P, and L/C during E. Cosmos DB never violates the specified consistency level, so it's formally CP. MongoDB can be classified as a PA/EC system. In the baseline case, the system guarantees reads and writes to be consistent. PNUTS is a PC/EL system. Hazelcast IMDG and indeed most in-memory data grids are an implementation of a PA/EC system; Hazelcast can be configured to be EL rather than EC. [8] Concurrency primitives (Lock, AtomicReference, CountDownLatch, etc.) can be either PC/EC or PA/EC. [9] FaunaDB implements Calvin, a transaction protocol created by Dr. Daniel Abadi, the author [3] of the original PACELC paper, and offers users adjustable controls for LC tradeoff. It is PC/EC for strictly serializable transactions, and EL for serializable reads. Source: Paxos Made Simple
URL: https://www.cs.uic.edu/~ajayk/paxosfinal.pdf
Paxos Made Simple Presented by Nakul Bhasin Rahul Choudhari Dharmesh Jagadish Paxos Algorithm given by Leslie Lamport in 2001. Paxos is a family of protocols for solving consensus in a network of unreliable processors (a fault tolerant distributed system). Consensus Algorithm A consensus algorithm ensures that a single one among the proposed values is chosen. If no value is proposed, then no value should be chosen. If a value has been chosen, then all the other processes should be able to learn the chosen value. Safety Requirements for Consensus Only a value that has been proposed may be chosen. Only a single value is chosen A process never learns that a value has been chosen unless it actually has been. Goal of Consensus algorithm Ensure that some proposed value is eventually chosen If a value has been chosen, then a process can eventually learn the value. Roles in the consensus algorithm Three roles in the consensus algorithm performed by three classes of agents Proposers- propose a value to be chosen Acceptors- decide which value to choose Learners- learn which value was chosen A single process may act as more than one agent. Agents can communicate by sending messages Assumptions in Model asynchronous & non-Byzantine model
Agents operate at arbitrary speed.
Agents may fail after a value is chosen and restarted
Messages:
can take  long to be delivered
can be duplicated
can be lost
but not corrupted. Approaches for Choosing a Value Single Acceptor(Easiest way): choose a value to have a single acceptor agent. A proposer sends a proposal to the acceptor. The acceptor  who chooses the first proposed value that it receives. . Choosing a value (contd.) Using multiple acceptor agents: Proposer sends a proposed value to a set of acceptors. An acceptor may accept the proposed value. The value is chosen when a large enough set of acceptors have accepted it. Requirement P1 Paxos should work even if only one proposal  is ever made (ignoring message loss). P1: An acceptor must accept the first proposal that it receives. But what if multiple proposals are made simultaneously? There might not be a majority of acceptors that accepted the same   proposal. Therefore acceptors must be able to accept more than one proposal. P1 isn't enough. Solution for P1 keeping track of the different proposal: < proposal number ; value > Different proposals have different numbers. Value chosen when single proposal with that value accepted by a majority of the acceptors Guarantees safety property that only a single value is chosen Allow multiple proposals to be chosen Requirement P2 We don't really need to require that only one proposal is chosen, as long as: P2:If a proposal with value v is chosen, then every higher-numbered proposal that is chosen has value v. condition P2 guarantees that only a single value is chosen Requirement P2a To be chosen, a proposal must be accepted by at least one acceptor. So, we can satisfy P2 by satisfying: P2a: If a proposal with value v is chosen, then every higher numbered proposal accepted by any acceptor has value v. If P2a holds, then P2 holds Requirement P2b Maintaining both P1 and P2a requires strengthening P2a to: P2b: If a proposal with value v is chosen, then every higher-numbered proposal issued by any proposer has value v. If P2b holds, then P2a must hold ,i.e., P2b  P2a P2 P2c: For any v and n, if a proposal with value v and number n is issued then there is a set S consisting of a majority of acceptors such that either (a) no acceptor in S has accepted any proposal numbered less than n or (b) v is the value of the highest-numbered proposal among all proposals numbered less than n accepted by the acceptors in S Proposers Algorithm STEP 1:  Prepare request: Send by the proposer with proposal number n
to each member of some set of acceptors, asking it to respond with: (a) A promise never again to accept a proposal numbered less than n, and (b) The proposal with the highest number less than n that it has accepted. Proposers Algorithm contd: Step 2 : proposer receives the responses from a majority of the acceptors, issue a proposal with number n and value v v->value of the highest-numbered proposal among the responses This is an accept request send by proposer What Acceptors do receive two kinds of requests from proposers: prepare requests and accept requests It can always respond to a prepare request It can respond to an accept request, if the following requirement is satisfied: P1a :An acceptor can accept a proposal
numbered n iff it has not responded to a prepare request having a number greater than n. Together, P1a and P2c give us the consensus algorithm. P2c tells proposers what proposals they can issue. P1a tells acceptors what proposals they can accept. Only one value can ever be chosen. Only a majority of acceptors need to accept a proposal to choose that value. Message Flow in Paxos Client Proposer Acceptor Learner X -------------------->     Request X ---------------> |X|X|X| Prepare (N) |<------------------X-X-X Promise(N,Va,Vb,Vc) X ----------------->|->|->| Accept(N, Vn) |<-----------------X-X-X------------------>|->| Accepted(N,Vn) <-------------------------------------------------------------------------X---X        Response Paxos Algorithm phase 1 Algorithm has two phases: Phase 1 [prepare request] Proposer chooses number n; sends a request to some majority of acceptors asking each for: (a) a promise not to accept proposals < n, and (b) the highest-numbered proposal < n that it has already accepted, if any Paxos Algorithm: phase 2 Phase 2 [accept phase] If majority of acceptors respond, issue proposal n whose value is: (a) the value of the highest-numbered proposal among the responses, or (b) anything we want if there weren't any such proposals. Message Flow in Paxos Client Proposer Acceptor Learner X -------------------->     Request X ---------------> |X|X|X| Prepare (N) |<------------------X-X-X Promise(N,Va,Vb,Vc) X ----------------->|->|->| Accept(N, Vn) |<-----------------X-X-X------------------>|->| Accepted(N,Vn) <-------------------------------------------------------------------------X---X        Response Example Proposers are p1 and p2. Acceptors are a1, a2, and a3. p1 sends prepare for proposal 1 to a1 and a2. a and a reply to p .1 2 1 p2 sends prepare for proposal 2 to a2 and a3. a2 and a3 reply to p2. Example (contd.) p1 sends accept request to a1 and a2 for proposal 1 with value “X” – p1 got to select which value to propose. a accepts proposal 1.1 a2 does not accept proposal 1. – a2 promised p2 it wouldn't accept proposals < 2 Example contd. p2 sends accept request to a2 and a3 for proposal 2 with value “Y” – p2 also got to select which value to propose. a accepts proposal 2.2 a3 accepts proposal 2. {a2, a3} is a majority of acceptors, so proposal 2 is chosen. – The chosen value is “Y” Example contd. p1 sends prepare for proposal 3 to a1 and a2. a1 replies; it last accepted proposal 1 for “X”. a2 replies; it last accepted proposal 2 for “Y”. p1 sends accept request to a1 and a2 for proposal 3 with value “Y” – Value must match the one from proposal 2. a1 and a2 accept proposal 3. Learning a chosen value How Do Learners Learn? Each acceptor informs each learner. nresponses = nlearners x nacceptors Each acceptor informs a distinguished learner, who relays to the other learners acceptors respond with their acceptances to a distinguished Learner nresponses = nlearners + nacceptors Learning contd. assumption of non-Byzantine failures requires lots of messages to be sent extra round required for all the learners to discover the chosen value single point of failure less reliable, since the distinguished learner could fail Learner algorithm (contd.) hybrid solution: set of distinguished learners Compromise with a set of distinguished Learners – Limits number of messages needed. – All distinguished learners need to fail to cause a problem. Optimization acceptor ignore to respond to a prepare request numbered n when a prepare m, with m > n has been already responded. It should ignore a prepare request for a proposal it has already accepted An acceptor should inform a proposal when it has delivered a proposal, while the acceptor already responded to an higher one. Progress Solution: allow only a distinguished proposer to prepare and issue proposals. – Proposers send their proposals to the distinguished proposer, who organizes them. – Doubles as distinguished learner. Can we avoid single-point-of-failure? – New distinguished proposer is elected if    the current one fails. – Two or more processes can think they're distinguished without compromising correctness (but can prevent progress). Implementation Each process plays the role of proposer, acceptor, and learner Leader election for the distinguished proposer and the distinguished learner An acceptor records its intended response in stable storage before actually sending the response. Implementation contd. Guarantee that no two proposals are ever issued with the same number -> every proposer choose their numbers from disjoint sets of numbers -> each proposer remembers (in stable storage) the highest-numbered proposal it has tried to issue, and begins phase 1 with a higher proposal number than any it has already used. So what is it good for? Is a consensus on a single arbitrary value actually useful? Yes! The Paxos consensus algorithm can be used to construct a distributed system of state machines. Distributed system: collection of clients issuing commands to central server. If single central server fails, system fails. State machine approach: implement fault- State Machine tolerant service by replicating servers and coordinating client interactions with server replicas.
Place State Machine copies on multiple, independent servers.
Receive client requests (Inputs) to State Machine.
Choose ordering for Inputs. Implementing a State Machine
Execute Inputs in chosen order on each server.
Respond to clients with Output from the State Machine.
Monitor replicas for differences in State or Output. Paxos can be used to implement distributed state machine:
values being agreed upon are commands to execute (commands by client).
one instance of Paxos executed for each
Implementing a State Machine command (value chosen by ith instance is the ith state machine command).
infinite number of instances executed simultaneously.
leader is elected to be the distinguished proposer and distinguished learner. Isn't Infinity Too Big? Phase 1 doesn‘t require knowing the value being proposed Leader can execute Phase 1 immediately, wait until something to propose for Phase 2. Phase 1 for all instances can be done with a single prepare request. E.g “this is a prepare request for proposal n for all instances > 30.” Implementing State Machine contd. If leader fails, new leader appointed. New leader already a learner in previous instances(first i instances) Sends prepare request for instances > i, uses same proposal no. If values had been chosen for any instances
i, the replies will include those proposals. State Machine Example New leader knows commands 1-134, 138, and 139. Sends prepare requests for instances 135-137 and 140+. Receives replies with existing proposals for instances 135 and 140. Issues accept requests for instances 135 and 140 with the appropriate values. Filling in the Gaps There may be “gaps” -- instances where no value had been proposed. – State machines can't execute command i
until all commands < i executed Leader chooses values to propose to fill those gaps in. Safest choice: no-op command. State Machine Example (2) Commands 136, 137, and 141+ are still undecided. To fill the gap, leader issues accept requests for instances 136 and 137 with a value of “no-op”. When the next command request arrives, the leader issues an accept request for instance 141 with that command as the value. Then issue 142, 143, .... Application of Paxos Google Megastore used for 3 billion writes and 20 billion read transactions daily. It uses Paxos to manage synchronous replication between datacenters. Provides the highest level of availability for reads and writes at the cost of higher-latency writes. Paxos is also used to perform write operations. Conclusion Safety is a guarantee in Paxos despite asynchrony. Once a the distinguish leader is elected, liveness is guaranteed. According to paper: phase 2 has the minimum possible cost of any algorithm for reaching agreement in presence of faults.
